**James Buchanan Jr.** ([/bjuːˈkænən/](/wiki/Help:IPA/English "Help:IPA/English") [_bew-KAN\-ən_](/wiki/Help:Pronunciation%5Frespelling%5Fkey "Help:Pronunciation respelling key");[\[3\]](#cite%5Fnote-3) April 23, 1791 – June 1, 1868) was the 15th [president of the United States](/wiki/President%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "President of the United States"), serving from 1857 to 1861\. Buchanan also served as the [secretary of state](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FSecretary%5Fof%5FState "United States Secretary of State") from 1845 to 1849 and represented [Pennsylvania](/wiki/Pennsylvania "Pennsylvania") in both houses of the [U.S. Congress](/wiki/U.S.%5FCongress "U.S. Congress"). He was an advocate for [states' rights](/wiki/States%27%5Frights "States' rights"), particularly regarding [slavery](/wiki/Slavery%5Fin%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Slavery in the United States"), and minimized the role of the [federal government](/wiki/Federal%5Fgovernment%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Federal government of the United States") preceding the [Civil War](/wiki/American%5FCivil%5FWar "American Civil War").

Buchanan was a lawyer in Pennsylvania and won his first election to the state's [House of Representatives](/wiki/Pennsylvania%5FHouse%5Fof%5FRepresentatives "Pennsylvania House of Representatives") as a [Federalist](/wiki/Federalist%5FParty "Federalist Party"). He was elected to the [U.S. House of Representatives](/wiki/U.S.%5FHouse%5Fof%5FRepresentatives "U.S. House of Representatives") in 1820 and retained that post for five terms, aligning with [Andrew Jackson](/wiki/Andrew%5FJackson "Andrew Jackson")'s [Democratic Party](/wiki/Democratic%5FParty%5F%28United%5FStates%29 "Democratic Party (United States)"). Buchanan served as Jackson's [minister to Russia](/wiki/List%5Fof%5Fambassadors%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fto%5FRussia "List of ambassadors of the United States to Russia") in 1832\. He won the election in 1834 as a [U.S. senator from Pennsylvania](/wiki/U.S.%5Fsenator%5Ffrom%5FPennsylvania "U.S. senator from Pennsylvania") and continued in that position for 11 years. He was appointed to serve as President [James K. Polk](/wiki/James%5FK.%5FPolk "James K. Polk")'s secretary of state in 1845, and eight years later was named as President [Franklin Pierce](/wiki/Franklin%5FPierce "Franklin Pierce")'s [minister to the United Kingdom](/wiki/List%5Fof%5Fambassadors%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fto%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FKingdom "List of ambassadors of the United States to the United Kingdom").

Beginning in 1844, Buchanan became a regular contender for the Democratic Party's presidential nomination. He was nominated and won the [1856 presidential election](/wiki/1856%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fpresidential%5Felection "1856 United States presidential election"). As President, Buchanan intervened to assure the [Supreme Court's](/wiki/Supreme%5FCourt%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Supreme Court of the United States") majority ruling in the pro-slavery decision in the [_Dred Scott_](/wiki/Dred%5FScott%5Fv.%5FSandford "Dred Scott v. Sandford") case. He acceded to Southern attempts to engineer [Kansas'](/wiki/Kansas%5FTerritory "Kansas Territory") entry into the Union as a [slave state](/wiki/Slave%5Fstates%5Fand%5Ffree%5Fstates "Slave states and free states") under the [Lecompton Constitution](/wiki/Lecompton%5FConstitution "Lecompton Constitution"), and angered not only Republicans but also Northern Democrats. Buchanan honored his pledge to serve only one term and supported Breckinridge's unsuccessful candidacy in the [1860 presidential election](/wiki/1860%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fpresidential%5Felection "1860 United States presidential election"). He failed to reconcile the fractured Democratic Party amid the grudge against [Stephen Douglas](/wiki/Stephen%5FDouglas "Stephen Douglas"), leading to the election of Republican and former Congressman [Abraham Lincoln](/wiki/Abraham%5FLincoln "Abraham Lincoln").

Buchanan's leadership during his [lame duck](/wiki/Lame%5Fduck%5F%28politics%29 "Lame duck (politics)") period, before the American Civil War, has been widely criticized. He simultaneously angered the North by not stopping secession and the South by not yielding to their demands. He supported the [Corwin Amendment](/wiki/Corwin%5FAmendment "Corwin Amendment") in an effort to reconcile the country. He made an unsuccessful attempt to reinforce [Fort Sumter](/wiki/Fort%5FSumter "Fort Sumter"), but otherwise refrained from preparing the military. In his personal life, Buchanan never married and was the only U.S. president to remain a [lifelong bachelor](/wiki/Bachelor "Bachelor"), leading some historians and authors to question his sexual orientation. His failure to forestall the [Civil War](/wiki/American%5FCivil%5FWar "American Civil War") has been described as incompetence, and he spent his last years defending his reputation. Historians and scholars [rank](/wiki/Historical%5Frankings%5Fof%5Fpresidents%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Historical rankings of presidents of the United States") Buchanan as among the worst presidents in American history.

## Early life

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Early life")\]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Buchanan_Home_Mercersburg.jpg/220px-Buchanan_Home_Mercersburg.jpg)](/wiki/File:Buchanan%5FHome%5FMercersburg.jpg)

Buchanan's birthplace

### Childhood and education

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Childhood and education")\]

James Buchanan Jr. was born into a [Scottish-Irish](/wiki/Scottish-Irish%5FAmericans "Scottish-Irish Americans") family on April 23, 1791, in a log cabin on a farm called [Stony Batter](/wiki/Buchanan%27s%5FBirthplace%5FState%5FPark "Buchanan's Birthplace State Park"), near [Cove Gap](/wiki/Cove%5FGap,%5FPennsylvania "Cove Gap, Pennsylvania"), [Peters Township](/wiki/Peters%5FTownship,%5FFranklin%5FCounty,%5FPennsylvania "Peters Township, Franklin County, Pennsylvania"), in the [Allegheny Mountains](/wiki/Allegheny%5FMountains "Allegheny Mountains") of southern [Pennsylvania](/wiki/Pennsylvania "Pennsylvania"). He was the last president born in the 18th century and, until the election of [Joe Biden](/wiki/Joe%5FBiden "Joe Biden") in 2020, the only one born in Pennsylvania.[\[4\]](#cite%5Fnote-4) Buchanan was the second of eleven children with six sisters and four brothers, and the eldest son of James Buchanan Sr. (1761–1821) and his wife Elizabeth Speer (1767–1833).[\[5\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker20049–12-5) James Buchanan Sr., was an [Ulster-Scot](/wiki/Ulster%5FScots%5Fpeople "Ulster Scots people") from just outside [Ramelton](/wiki/Ramelton "Ramelton"), a small town in the north-east of [County Donegal](/wiki/County%5FDonegal "County Donegal") in the north-west of [Ulster](/wiki/Ulster "Ulster"), the northern [province](/wiki/Provinces%5Fof%5FIreland "Provinces of Ireland") in [Ireland](/wiki/Ireland "Ireland"), who emigrated to the newly formed [United States](/wiki/United%5FStates "United States") in 1783, having sailed from [Derry](/wiki/Derry "Derry").[\[6\]](#cite%5Fnote-6)[\[7\]](#cite%5Fnote-7) He belonged to the [Clan Buchanan](/wiki/Clan%5FBuchanan "Clan Buchanan"), whose members had emigrated in large numbers from the [Scottish Highlands](/wiki/Scottish%5FHighlands "Scottish Highlands") to [Ulster](/wiki/Ulster "Ulster") in the north of Ireland during the [Plantation of Ulster](/wiki/Plantation%5Fof%5FUlster "Plantation of Ulster") in the seventeenth century and, later, largely because of poverty and persecution by [the Crown](/wiki/The%5FCrown "The Crown") due to their [Presbyterian](/wiki/Presbyterianism "Presbyterianism") faith, had further emigrated in large numbers from Ulster to America from the early eighteenth century onwards. Shortly after Buchanan's birth, the family relocated to a farm near [Mercersburg, Pennsylvania](/wiki/Mercersburg,%5FPennsylvania "Mercersburg, Pennsylvania"), and later settled in the town in 1794\. His father became the area's wealthiest resident, working as a merchant, farmer, and real estate investor. Buchanan attributed his early education primarily to his mother, whereas his father had a greater influence on his character. His mother had discussed politics with him as a child and had an interest in poetry, quoting [John Milton](/wiki/John%5FMilton "John Milton") and [William Shakespeare](/wiki/William%5FShakespeare "William Shakespeare") to Buchanan.[\[5\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker20049–12-5) 

Buchanan attended the Old Stone Academy in Mercersburg and then [Dickinson College](/wiki/Dickinson%5FCollege "Dickinson College") in [Carlisle, Pennsylvania](/wiki/Carlisle,%5FPennsylvania "Carlisle, Pennsylvania").[\[8\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200412-8) In 1808, he was nearly expelled for disorderly conduct; he and his fellow students had attracted negative attention for drinking in local taverns, disturbing the peace at night and committing acts of vandalism,[\[9\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200413–16-9) but he pleaded for a second chance and ultimately graduated with honors in 1809.[\[10\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein19629–12-10) Later that year, he moved to the state capital at [Lancaster](/wiki/Lancaster,%5FPennsylvania "Lancaster, Pennsylvania"), to train as a lawyer for two and a half years with the well-known James Hopkins. Following the fashion of the time, Buchanan studied the [United States Code](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FCode "United States Code") and the [Constitution of the United States](/wiki/Constitution%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Constitution of the United States") as well as legal authorities such as [William Blackstone](/wiki/William%5FBlackstone "William Blackstone") during his education.[\[9\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200413–16-9) 

### Early law practice and Pennsylvania House of Representatives

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Early law practice and Pennsylvania House of Representatives")\]

In 1812, Buchanan passed the bar exam and after being admitted to the bar, he remained in Lancaster, even when [Harrisburg](/wiki/Harrisburg,%5FPennsylvania "Harrisburg, Pennsylvania") became the new capital of Pennsylvania. Buchanan quickly established himself as a prominent legal representative in the city. His income rapidly rose after he established his practice, and by 1821 he was earning over $11,000 per year (equivalent to $250,000 in 2023).[\[9\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200413–16-9) At this time, Buchanan became a [Freemason](/wiki/Freemason "Freemason"), and served as the Worshipful Master of [Masonic Lodge](/wiki/Masonic%5FLodge "Masonic Lodge") No. 43 in Lancaster and as a District Deputy [Grand Master](/wiki/Master%5FMason "Master Mason") of the [Grand Lodge of Pennsylvania](/wiki/Grand%5FLodge%5Fof%5FPennsylvania "Grand Lodge of Pennsylvania").[\[11\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein196227-11) 

Buchanan also served as chairman of the Lancaster chapter of the [Federalist Party](/wiki/Federalist%5FParty "Federalist Party"). Like his father, he supported their political program, which provided federal funds for building projects and import duties as well as the re-establishment of a central bank after the [First Bank of the United States](/wiki/First%5FBank%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "First Bank of the United States")' license expired in 1811\. He became a strong critic of [Democratic-Republican](/wiki/Democratic-Republican%5FParty "Democratic-Republican Party") President [James Madison](/wiki/James%5FMadison "James Madison") during the [War of 1812](/wiki/War%5Fof%5F1812 "War of 1812").[\[12\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200417–18-12) Although he did not himself serve in a militia during the War of 1812, during the British occupation he joined a group of young men who stole horses for the [United States Army](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FArmy "United States Army") in the [Baltimore](/wiki/Baltimore "Baltimore") area.[\[13\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein196217–18-13) He was the last president involved in the [War of 1812](/wiki/War%5Fof%5F1812 "War of 1812").[\[14\]](#cite%5Fnote-14) 

In 1814, he was elected for the Federalists to the [Pennsylvania House of Representatives](/wiki/Pennsylvania%5FHouse%5Fof%5FRepresentatives "Pennsylvania House of Representatives"),[\[15\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTECurtis188322-15) where he was the youngest member, and held this seat until 1816\. Since the sessions in the [Pennsylvania General Assembly](/wiki/Pennsylvania%5FGeneral%5FAssembly "Pennsylvania General Assembly") lasted only three months, Buchanan continued practicing law at a profit by charging higher fees, and his service helped him acquire more clients.[\[16\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200418-16) In 1815, Buchanan defended District Judge [Walter Franklin](/wiki/Walter%5FFranklin%5F%28judge%29 "Walter Franklin (judge)") in an [impeachment trial](/wiki/Impeachment%5Ftrial "Impeachment trial") before the [Pennsylvania Senate](/wiki/Pennsylvania%5FSenate "Pennsylvania Senate"), over alleged judicial misconduct. Impeachments were more common at the time because the line between abuse of office and a wrong legal decision was determined by the ruling parties' preferences and the popularity of the judge's decision. Buchanan persuaded the senators that only judicial crimes and clear violations of the law justified impeachment.[\[12\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200417–18-12) 

## Congressional career

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Congressional career")\]

Main article: [Electoral history of James Buchanan](/wiki/Electoral%5Fhistory%5Fof%5FJames%5FBuchanan "Electoral history of James Buchanan")

### U.S. House of Representatives

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: U.S. House of Representatives")\]

In the congressional elections of 1820, Buchanan ran for a seat in the [House of Representatives](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FHouse%5Fof%5FRepresentatives "United States House of Representatives"). Shortly after his election victory, his father died in a carriage accident.[\[17\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200422-17) As a young Representative, Buchanan was one of the most prominent leaders of the "Amalgamator party" faction of Pennsylvanian politics, named that because it was made up of both Democratic-Republicans and former Federalists, which transitioned from the [First Party System](/wiki/First%5FParty%5FSystem "First Party System") to the [Era of Good Feelings](/wiki/Era%5Fof%5FGood%5FFeelings "Era of Good Feelings"). During this era, the Democratic-Republicans became the most influential party. Buchanan's Federalist convictions were weak, and he switched parties after opposing a nativist Federalist bill.[\[18\]](#cite%5Fnote-:0-18) During the [1824 presidential election](/wiki/1824%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fpresidential%5Felection "1824 United States presidential election"), Buchanan initially supported [Henry Clay](/wiki/Henry%5FClay "Henry Clay"), but switched to Andrew Jackson (with Clay as a second choice) when it became clear that the Pennsylvanian public overwhelmingly preferred Jackson.[\[19\]](#cite%5Fnote-19) After Jackson lost the 1824 election, he joined his faction, but Jackson had contempt for Buchanan due to his misinterpretation of his efforts to mediate between the Clay and Jackson camps.[\[18\]](#cite%5Fnote-:0-18) 

In Washington, Buchanan became an avid defender of [states' rights](/wiki/States%27%5Frights "States' rights"), and was close with many southern Congressmen, viewing some New England Congressmen as dangerous [radicals](/wiki/Radical%5Fpolitics "Radical politics"). Buchanan's close proximity to his constituency allowed him to establish a Democratic coalition in Pennsylvania, consisting of former Federalist farmers, Philadelphia artisans, and Ulster-Scots-Americans. In the 1828 presidential election, he secured Pennsylvania, while the "[Jacksonian Democrats](/wiki/Jacksonian%5Fdemocracy "Jacksonian democracy")", an independent party after splitting from the [National Republican Party](/wiki/National%5FRepublican%5FParty "National Republican Party"), won an easy victory in the parallel congressional election.[\[20\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200424–27-20) 

Buchanan gained most attention during an impeachment trial where he acted as prosecutor for federal district judge [James H. Peck](/wiki/James%5FH.%5FPeck "James H. Peck"); however, the Senate rejected Buchanan's plea and acquitted Peck by a majority vote. He was appointed to the [Agriculture Committee](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FSenate%5FCommittee%5Fon%5FAgriculture,%5FNutrition,%5Fand%5FForestry "United States Senate Committee on Agriculture, Nutrition, and Forestry") in his first year, and he eventually became chairman of the [Judiciary Committee](/wiki/U.S.%5FHouse%5FCommittee%5Fon%5Fthe%5FJudiciary "U.S. House Committee on the Judiciary"). In 1831, Buchanan declined a nomination for the [22nd United States Congress](/wiki/22nd%5FUnited%5FStates%5FCongress "22nd United States Congress") from his constituency consisting of [Dauphin](/wiki/Dauphin%5FCounty,%5FPennsylvania "Dauphin County, Pennsylvania"), [Lebanon](/wiki/Lebanon%5FCounty,%5FPennsylvania "Lebanon County, Pennsylvania"), and Lancaster counties. He still had political ambitions and some Pennsylvania Democrats put him forward as a candidate for the vice presidency in the [1832 election](/wiki/1832%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fpresidential%5Felection "1832 United States presidential election").[\[21\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200428–30-21)

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/James_Buchanan_painted_by_J._Eichholtz.jpg/220px-James_Buchanan_painted_by_J._Eichholtz.jpg)](/wiki/File:James%5FBuchanan%5Fpainted%5Fby%5FJ.%5FEichholtz.jpg)

1834 portrait of Buchanan at age 42–43 by [Jacob Eichholtz](/wiki/Jacob%5FEichholtz "Jacob Eichholtz")

### Minister to Russia

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: Minister to Russia")\]

After Jackson was re-elected in 1832, he offered Buchanan the position of [United States Ambassador to Russia](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FAmbassador%5Fto%5FRussia "United States Ambassador to Russia"). Buchanan was reluctant to leave the country, as the distant [St. Petersburg](/wiki/Saint%5FPetersburg "Saint Petersburg") was a kind of political exile, which was the intention of Jackson, who considered Buchanan to be an "incompetent busybody" and untrustworthy, but he ultimately agreed.[\[18\]](#cite%5Fnote-:0-18) His work focused on concluding a trade and shipping treaty with Russia. While Buchanan was successful with the former, negotiating an agreement on free merchant shipping with Foreign Minister [Karl Nesselrode](/wiki/Karl%5FNesselrode "Karl Nesselrode") proved difficult.[\[22\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200430–31-22) He had denounced Tsar [Nicholas I](/wiki/Nicholas%5FI%5Fof%5FRussia "Nicholas I of Russia") as a despot merely a year prior during his tenure in Congress; many Americans had reacted negatively to Russia's reaction to the [1830 Polish uprising](/wiki/November%5FUprising "November Uprising").[\[23\]](#cite%5Fnote-23) 

### U.S. Senator

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=7 "Edit section: U.S. Senator")\]

Buchanan returned home and lost the election in the [State Legislature](/wiki/State%5Flegislature%5F%28United%5FStates%29 "State legislature (United States)") for a full six-year term in the [23rd Congress](/wiki/23rd%5FUnited%5FStates%5FCongress "23rd United States Congress"), but was appointed by the Pennsylvania state legislature to succeed [William Wilkins](/wiki/William%5FWilkins%5F%28U.S.%5Fpolitician%29 "William Wilkins (U.S. politician)") in the U.S. Senate. Wilkins, in turn, replaced Buchanan as the ambassador to Russia. The Jacksonian Buchanan, who was re-elected in 1836 and 1842, opposed the re-chartering of the [Second Bank of the United States](/wiki/Second%5FBank%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Second Bank of the United States") and sought to expunge a congressional censure of Jackson stemming from the [Bank War](/wiki/Bank%5FWar "Bank War").[\[24\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200430-24) Buchanan served in the Senate until March 1845 and was twice confirmed in office.[\[25\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200432-25) To unite Pennsylvania Democrats at the State Convention, he was chosen as their candidate for the [National Convention](/wiki/Democratic%5FNational%5FConvention "Democratic National Convention"). Buchanan maintained a strict adherence to the [Pennsylvania State Legislature](/wiki/Pennsylvania%5FState%5FLegislature "Pennsylvania State Legislature")'s guidelines and sometimes voted against positions in Congress which he promoted in his own speeches, despite open ambitions for the White House.[\[26\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200435–38-26) 

Buchanan was known for his commitment to [states' rights](/wiki/States%27%5Frights "States' rights") and the [Manifest Destiny](/wiki/Manifest%5Fdestiny "Manifest destiny") ideology.[\[27\]](#cite%5Fnote-27) He rejected President [Martin Van Buren](/wiki/Martin%5FVan%5FBuren "Martin Van Buren")'s offer to become [United States Attorney General](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FAttorney%5FGeneral "United States Attorney General") and chaired prestigious Senate committees such as the [Committee on the Judiciary](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FHouse%5FCommittee%5Fon%5Fthe%5FJudiciary "United States House Committee on the Judiciary") and the [Committee on Foreign Relations](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FSenate%5FCommittee%5Fon%5FForeign%5FRelations "United States Senate Committee on Foreign Relations").[\[28\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200433–34-28) Buchanan was one of only a few senators to vote against the [Webster–Ashburton Treaty](/wiki/Webster%E2%80%93Ashburton%5FTreaty "Webster–Ashburton Treaty") for its "surrender" of lands to the United Kingdom, as he demanded the entire [Aroostook River Valley](/wiki/Aroostook%5FRiver "Aroostook River") for the United States. In the [Oregon Boundary Dispute](/wiki/Oregon%5Fboundary%5Fdispute "Oregon boundary dispute"), Buchanan adopted the maximum demand of 54°40′ as the northern border and spoke out in favor of annexing the [Republic of Texas](/wiki/Republic%5Fof%5FTexas "Republic of Texas").[\[26\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200435–38-26) During the contentious [1838 Pennsylvania gubernatorial election](/wiki/1838%5FPennsylvania%5Fgubernatorial%5Felection "1838 Pennsylvania gubernatorial election"), Buchanan chose to support the Democratic challenger, [David Rittenhouse Porter](/wiki/David%5FR.%5FPorter "David R. Porter"),[\[29\]](#cite%5Fnote-29) who was elected by fewer than 5,500 votes as Pennsylvania's first governor under the state's revised Constitution of 1838.[\[30\]](#cite%5Fnote-30)[\[31\]](#cite%5Fnote-31) 

Buchanan also opposed a [gag rule](/wiki/Gag%5Frule%5F%28United%5FStates%29 "Gag rule (United States)") sponsored by [John C. Calhoun](/wiki/John%5FC.%5FCalhoun "John C. Calhoun") that would have suppressed anti-slavery petitions. He joined the majority in blocking the rule, with most senators of the belief that it would have the reverse effect of strengthening the [abolitionists](/wiki/Abolitionist "Abolitionist").[\[32\]](#cite%5Fnote-32) He said, "We have just as little right to interfere with slavery in the South, as we have to touch the right of petition."[\[25\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200432-25) Buchanan thought that the issue of [slavery](/wiki/Slavery%5Fin%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Slavery in the United States") was the domain of the states, and he faulted abolitionists for exciting passions over the issue. In the lead-up to the [1844 Democratic National Convention](/wiki/1844%5FDemocratic%5FNational%5FConvention "1844 Democratic National Convention"), Buchanan positioned himself as a potential alternative to former President [Martin Van Buren](/wiki/Martin%5FVan%5FBuren "Martin Van Buren"), but the nomination went to [James K. Polk](/wiki/James%5FK.%5FPolk "James K. Polk"), who won the [election](/wiki/1844%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fpresidential%5Felection "1844 United States presidential election").[\[26\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200435–38-26) 

## Diplomatic career

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=8 "Edit section: Diplomatic career")\]

### Secretary of State

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: Secretary of State")\]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Polkcabinet1849edited.png/220px-Polkcabinet1849edited.png)](/wiki/File:Polkcabinet1849edited.png)

Buchanan (second from the left) in Polk's cabinet, 1849

Buchanan was offered the position of [Secretary of State](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FSecretary%5Fof%5FState "United States Secretary of State") in the Polk administration or, as the alternative, a seat on the [Supreme Court](/wiki/Supreme%5FCourt%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Supreme Court of the United States"), to compensate him for his support in the election campaign but also in order to eliminate him as an internal party rival. He accepted the State Department post and served for the duration of Polk's single term in office. During his tenure, the United States recorded its largest territorial gain in history through the [Oregon Treaty](/wiki/Oregon%5FTreaty "Oregon Treaty") and the [Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo](/wiki/Treaty%5Fof%5FGuadalupe%5FHidalgo "Treaty of Guadalupe Hidalgo"), which included territory that is now Texas, California, Nevada, New Mexico, Arizona, Utah, and Colorado.[\[33\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200438–40-33) In negotiations with Britain over Oregon, Buchanan initially favored the [49th parallel](/wiki/49th%5Fparallel%5Fnorth "49th parallel north") as the boundary of Oregon Territory, while Polk called for a more northerly boundary line. When Northern Democrats rallied around the popular slogan [Fifty-Four Forty or Fight](/wiki/Fifty-Four%5FForty%5For%5FFight "Fifty-Four Forty or Fight") ("54°40′ or war") in the 1844 election campaign, Buchanan adopted this position, but later followed Polk's direction, leading to the Oregon Compromise of 1846, which established the 49th parallel as the boundary in the [Pacific Northwest](/wiki/Pacific%5FNorthwest "Pacific Northwest").[\[34\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200440–41-34) 

In regards to Mexico, Buchanan maintained a dubious view that its attack on American troops on the other side of the [Rio Grande](/wiki/Rio%5FGrande "Rio Grande") in April 1846 constituted a border violation and a legitimate reason for war. During the [Mexican-American War](/wiki/Mexican%E2%80%93American%5FWar "Mexican–American War"), Buchanan initially advised against claiming territory south of the Rio Grande, fearing war with Britain and France. However, as the war came to an end, Buchanan changed his mind and argued for the annexation of further territory, arguing that Mexico was to blame for the war and that the compensation negotiated for the American losses was too low. Buchanan sought the nomination at the [1848 Democratic National Convention](/wiki/1848%5FDemocratic%5FNational%5FConvention "1848 Democratic National Convention"), as Polk had promised to serve only one term, but he only won the support of the Pennsylvania and Virginia delegations, so Senator [Lewis Cass](/wiki/Lewis%5FCass "Lewis Cass") of Michigan was nominated.[\[35\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200441–43-35) 

### Civilian life and 1852 presidential election

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Civilian life and 1852 presidential election")\]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/James_Buchanan_sculpture_at_National_Portrait_Gallery_IMG_4538.JPG/220px-James_Buchanan_sculpture_at_National_Portrait_Gallery_IMG_4538.JPG)](/wiki/File:James%5FBuchanan%5Fsculpture%5Fat%5FNational%5FPortrait%5FGallery%5FIMG%5F4538.JPG)

Bust of James Buchanan by [Henry Dexter](/wiki/Henry%5FDexter%5F%28sculptor%29 "Henry Dexter (sculptor)") at the National Portrait Gallery

With the [1848 election](/wiki/1848%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fpresidential%5Felection "1848 United States presidential election") of Whig [Zachary Taylor](/wiki/Zachary%5FTaylor "Zachary Taylor"), Buchanan returned to private life. Buchanan was getting on in years and still dressed in the old-fashioned style of his adolescence, earning him the nickname "[Old Public Functionary](/wiki/Old%5FPublic%5FFunctionary "Old Public Functionary")" from the press. Slavery opponents in the North mocked him as a relic of prehistoric man because of his moral values.[\[36\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200446–48-36) He bought the house of [Wheatland](/wiki/Wheatland%5F%28James%5FBuchanan%5FHouse%29 "Wheatland (James Buchanan House)") on the outskirts of Lancaster and entertained various visitors while monitoring political events.[\[37\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200443–46-37) During this period, Buchanan became the center of a family network consisting of 22 nieces, nephews and their descendants, seven of whom were orphans. He found public service jobs for some through patronage, and for those in his favor, he took on the role of surrogate father. He formed the strongest emotional bond with his niece [Harriet Lane](/wiki/Harriet%5FLane "Harriet Lane"), who later became First Lady for Buchanan in the White House.[\[36\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200446–48-36) 

In 1852, he was named president of the Board of Trustees of [Franklin and Marshall College](/wiki/Franklin%5Fand%5FMarshall%5FCollege "Franklin and Marshall College") in Lancaster, and he served in this capacity until 1866.[\[38\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962210,%5F415-38) Buchanan did not completely leave politics. He intended to publish a collection of speeches and an autobiography, but his political comeback was thwarted by the 1852 presidential election. Buchanan traveled to Washington to discuss Pennsylvania Democratic Party politics, which were divided into two camps led by [Simon Cameron](/wiki/Simon%5FCameron "Simon Cameron") and [George Dallas](/wiki/George%5FM.%5FDallas "George M. Dallas").[\[39\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200449–51-39) He quietly campaigned for the 1852 Democratic presidential nomination. In light of the [Compromise of 1850](/wiki/Compromise%5Fof%5F1850 "Compromise of 1850"), which had led to the admission of California into the Union as a free state and a stricter [Fugitive Slave Act](/wiki/Fugitive%5FSlave%5FAct%5Fof%5F1850 "Fugitive Slave Act of 1850"), Buchanan now rejected the [Missouri Compromise](/wiki/Missouri%5FCompromise "Missouri Compromise") and welcomed Congress's rejection of the [Wilmot Proviso](/wiki/Wilmot%5FProviso "Wilmot Proviso"), which prohibited slavery in all territories gained in the Mexican-American War. Buchanan criticized [abolitionism](/wiki/Abolitionism%5Fin%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Abolitionism in the United States") as a fanatical attitude and believed that slavery should be decided by state legislatures, not Congress. He disliked abolitionist Northerners due to his party affiliation, and became known as a "[doughface](/wiki/Doughface "Doughface")" due to his sympathy toward the South. Buchanan emerged as a promising candidate for the Democratic presidential nomination, alongside Lewis Cass, [Stephen Douglas](/wiki/Stephen%5FA.%5FDouglas "Stephen A. Douglas"), and [William L. Marcy](/wiki/William%5FL.%5FMarcy "William L. Marcy"); however, the Pennsylvania convention did not vote unanimously in his favor, with over 30 delegates protesting against him.[\[40\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200452–56-40) At the [1852 Democratic National Convention](/wiki/1852%5FDemocratic%5FNational%5FConvention "1852 Democratic National Convention"), he won the support of many southern delegates but failed to win the two-thirds support needed for the presidential nomination, which went to [Franklin Pierce](/wiki/Franklin%5FPierce "Franklin Pierce"). Buchanan declined to serve as the vice presidential nominee, and the convention instead nominated his close friend, [William R. King](/wiki/William%5FR.%5FKing "William R. King").[\[41\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200457–59-41) 

### Minister to the United Kingdom

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Minister to the United Kingdom")\]

Pierce won the election in [1852](/wiki/1852%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fpresidential%5Felection "1852 United States presidential election"), and six months later, Buchanan accepted the position of [United States Minister to the United Kingdom](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FMinister%5Fto%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FKingdom "United States Minister to the United Kingdom"), a position that represented a step backward in his career and that he had twice previously rejected.[\[41\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200457–59-41) Buchanan sailed for England in the summer of 1853, and he remained abroad for the next three years. In 1850, the United States and Great Britain signed the [Clayton–Bulwer Treaty](/wiki/Clayton%E2%80%93Bulwer%5FTreaty "Clayton–Bulwer Treaty"), which committed both countries to joint control of any future canal that would connect the Atlantic and Pacific Oceans through Central America. Buchanan met repeatedly with [Lord Clarendon](/wiki/George%5FVilliers,%5F4th%5FEarl%5Fof%5FClarendon "George Villiers, 4th Earl of Clarendon"), the British foreign minister, in hopes of pressuring the British to withdraw from Central America. He was able to reduce British influence in [Honduras](/wiki/Honduras "Honduras") and [Nicaragua](/wiki/Nicaragua "Nicaragua") while also raising the kingdom's awareness of American interests in the region.[\[42\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200465–67-42) He also focused on the potential annexation of [Cuba](/wiki/Cuba "Cuba"), which had long interested him.[\[43\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200458–64-43) 

At Pierce's prompting, Buchanan met in [Ostend, Belgium](/wiki/Ostend "Ostend"), with U.S. Ambassador to Spain [Pierre Soulé](/wiki/Pierre%5FSoul%C3%A9 "Pierre Soulé") and U.S. Ambassador to France [John Mason](/wiki/John%5FY.%5FMason "John Y. Mason"), to work out a plan for the acquisition of Cuba. A memorandum draft resulted, called the [Ostend Manifesto](/wiki/Ostend%5FManifesto "Ostend Manifesto"), which proposed the purchase of Cuba from Spain, then in the midst of revolution and near bankruptcy. The document declared the island "as necessary to the North American republic as any of its present ... family of states". Against Buchanan's recommendation, the final draft of the manifesto suggested that "wresting it from Spain", if Spain refused to sell, would be justified "by every law, human and Divine".[\[44\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988110-44) The manifesto was met with a divided response and was never acted upon. It weakened the Pierce administration and reduced support for Manifest Destiny.[\[44\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEMcPherson1988110-44)[\[45\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTETucker2009456–57-45) In 1855, as Buchanan's desire to return home grew, Pierce asked him to hold the fort in [London](/wiki/London "London") in light of the relocation of a British fleet to the [Caribbean](/wiki/Caribbean "Caribbean").[\[42\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200465–67-42) 

## Election of 1856

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Election of 1856")\]

Main article: [1856 United States presidential election](/wiki/1856%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fpresidential%5Felection "1856 United States presidential election")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/ElectoralCollege1856.svg/330px-ElectoralCollege1856.svg.png)](/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1856.svg)

1856 Map of electoral votes

Buchanan's service abroad allowed him to conveniently avoid the debate over the [Kansas–Nebraska Act](/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska%5FAct "Kansas–Nebraska Act") then roiling the country in the slavery dispute.[\[46\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200467–68-46) While he did not overtly seek the presidency, he assented to the movement on his behalf.[\[47\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962248–252-47) While still in England, he campaigned by praising [John Joseph Hughes](/wiki/John%5FHughes%5F%28archbishop%29 "John Hughes (archbishop)"), who was [Archbishop of New York](/wiki/Archbishop%5Fof%5FNew%5FYork "Archbishop of New York"), to a Catholic archbishop. The latter campaigned for Buchanan among high-ranking [Catholics](/wiki/Catholics "Catholics") as soon as he heard about it.[\[46\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200467–68-46) When Buchanan arrived home at the end of April 1856, he led on the first ballot, supported by powerful Senators [John Slidell](/wiki/John%5FSlidell "John Slidell"), [Jesse Bright](/wiki/Jesse%5FBright "Jesse Bright"), and [Thomas F. Bayard](/wiki/Thomas%5FF.%5FBayard "Thomas F. Bayard"), who presented Buchanan as an experienced leader appealing to the North and South. The [1856 Democratic National Convention](/wiki/1856%5FDemocratic%5FNational%5FConvention "1856 Democratic National Convention") met in June 1856, producing a platform that reflected Buchanan's views, including support for the [Fugitive Slave Law](/wiki/Fugitive%5FSlave%5FLaw "Fugitive Slave Law"), which required the return of escaped slaves. The platform also called for an end to anti-slavery agitation and U.S. "ascendancy in the [Gulf of Mexico](/wiki/Gulf%5Fof%5FMexico "Gulf of Mexico")".[\[48\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200469-48) President Pierce hoped for re-nomination, while Senator [Stephen A. Douglas](/wiki/Stephen%5FA.%5FDouglas "Stephen A. Douglas") also loomed as a strong candidate. He won the nomination after seventeen ballots after Douglas' resignation. He was joined on the ticket by [John C. Breckinridge](/wiki/John%5FC.%5FBreckinridge "John C. Breckinridge") of Kentucky in order to maintain regional proportional representation, placating supporters of Pierce and Douglas, also allies of Breckinridge.[\[49\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200469–70-49) 

Buchanan faced two candidates in the general election: former Whig President [Millard Fillmore](/wiki/Millard%5FFillmore "Millard Fillmore") ran as the candidate for the anti-Catholic, anti-immigrant American Party (or "[Know-Nothing](/wiki/Know-Nothing "Know-Nothing")"), while [John C. Frémont](/wiki/John%5FC.%5FFr%C3%A9mont "John C. Frémont") ran as the [Republican](/wiki/U.S.%5FRepublican%5FParty "U.S. Republican Party") nominee. The contrast between Buchanan and Frémont was particularly stark, with opposing caricaturists drawing the Democratic candidate as a fussy old man in drag.[\[50\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200470–73-50) Buchanan did not actively campaign, but he wrote letters and pledged to uphold the Democratic platform. In the election, he carried every slave state except for Maryland, as well as five slavery-free states, including his home state of Pennsylvania.[\[49\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200469–70-49) He won 45 percent of the popular vote and decisively won the electoral vote, taking 174 of 296 votes. His election made him the first president from Pennsylvania. In a combative victory speech, Buchanan denounced Republicans, calling them a "dangerous" and "geographical" party that had unfairly attacked the South.[\[50\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200470–73-50) He also declared, "the object of my administration will be to destroy sectional party, North or South, and to restore harmony to the Union under a national and conservative government."[\[51\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962261–262-51) He set about this initially by feigning a sectional balance in his cabinet appointments.[\[52\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200477–80-52) 

## Presidency (1857–1861)

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Presidency (1857–1861)")\]

Main article: [Presidency of James Buchanan](/wiki/Presidency%5Fof%5FJames%5FBuchanan "Presidency of James Buchanan")

### Inauguration

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Inauguration")\]

Main article: [Inauguration of James Buchanan](/wiki/Inauguration%5Fof%5FJames%5FBuchanan "Inauguration of James Buchanan")

Buchanan was inaugurated on March 4, 1857, taking the oath of office from [Chief Justice](/wiki/Chief%5FJustice%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Chief Justice of the United States") [Roger B. Taney](/wiki/Roger%5FB.%5FTaney "Roger B. Taney"). In his lengthy inaugural address, Buchanan committed himself to serving only one term, as his predecessor had done. He abhorred the growing divisions over slavery and its status in the territories, saying that Congress should play no role in determining the status of slavery in the states or territories.[\[53\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200480–83,%5F85-53) He proposed a solution based on the [Kansas-Nebraska Act](/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska%5FAct "Kansas–Nebraska Act"), which stated that the principle of [popular sovereignty](/wiki/Popular%5Fsovereignty%5Fin%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Popular sovereignty in the United States") was decisive, and Congress had no say in the matter. Buchanan recommended that a federal slave code be enacted to protect the rights of slaveowners in federal territories. He alluded to a then-pending Supreme Court case, _[Dred Scott v. Sandford](/wiki/Dred%5FScott%5Fv.%5FSandford "Dred Scott v. Sandford")_, which he said would permanently settle the issue of slavery. Dred Scott was a slave who was temporarily taken from a slave state to a free territory by his owner, [John Sanford](/wiki/John%5FF.%5FA.%5FSanford "John F. A. Sanford"). After Scott returned to the slave state, he filed a petition for his freedom based on his time in the free territory.[\[53\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200480–83,%5F85-53) 

Associate Justice [Robert C. Grier](/wiki/Robert%5FC.%5FGrier "Robert C. Grier") leaked the decision in the "Dred Scott" case early to Buchanan. In his inaugural address, Buchanan declared that the issue of slavery in the territories would be "speedily and finally settled" by the Supreme Court.[\[54\]](#cite%5Fnote-54) According to historian [Paul Finkelman](/wiki/Paul%5FFinkelman "Paul Finkelman"):

> Buchanan already knew what the Court was going to decide. In a major breach of Court etiquette, Justice Grier, who, like Buchanan, was from Pennsylvania, had kept the President-elect fully informed about the progress of the case and the internal debates within the Court. When Buchanan urged the nation to support the decision, he already knew what Taney would say. Republican suspicions of impropriety turned out to be fully justified.[\[55\]](#cite%5Fnote-55)

Historians agree that the court decision was a major disaster because it dramatically inflamed tensions, leading to the Civil War.[\[56\]](#cite%5Fnote-Carrafiello-A-56)[\[57\]](#cite%5Fnote-57)[\[58\]](#cite%5Fnote-58) In 2022, historian [David W. Blight](/wiki/David%5FW.%5FBlight "David W. Blight") argued that the year 1857 was, "the great pivot on the road to disunion...largely because of the Dred Scott case, which stoked the fear, distrust and conspiratorial hatred already common in both the North and the South to new levels of intensity."[\[59\]](#cite%5Fnote-59) 

### Personnel

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Personnel")\]
