[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Buchanan_Cabinet.jpg/220px-Buchanan_Cabinet.jpg)](/wiki/File:Buchanan%5FCabinet.jpg)

_President Buchanan and his Cabinet_, photograph by [Mathew Brady](/wiki/Mathew%5FBrady "Mathew Brady") (c. 1859).  
From left to right: [Jacob Thompson](/wiki/Jacob%5FThompson "Jacob Thompson"), [Lewis Cass](/wiki/Lewis%5FCass "Lewis Cass"), [John B. Floyd](/wiki/John%5FB.%5FFloyd "John B. Floyd"), James Buchanan, [Howell Cobb](/wiki/Howell%5FCobb "Howell Cobb"), [Isaac Toucey](/wiki/Isaac%5FToucey "Isaac Toucey"), [Joseph Holt](/wiki/Joseph%5FHolt "Joseph Holt") and [Jeremiah S. Black](/wiki/Jeremiah%5FS.%5FBlack "Jeremiah S. Black")

As his inauguration approached, Buchanan sought to establish an obedient, harmonious cabinet to avoid the in-fighting that had plagued [Andrew Jackson](/wiki/Andrew%5FJackson "Andrew Jackson")'s administration.[\[60\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200477-60) The cabinet's composition had to do justice to the proportional representation within the party and between the regions of the country. Buchanan first worked on this task in Wheatland until he traveled to the capital in January 1857\. There, like many other guests at the [National Hotel](/wiki/National%5FHotel%5Fdisease "National Hotel disease"), he contracted severe [dysentery](/wiki/Dysentery "Dysentery"), from which he did not fully recover until several months later. Dozens of those who fell ill died, including Buchanan's nephew and private secretary Eskridge Lane.[\[52\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200477–80-52) 

The cabinet selection was disastrous, with four Southern ministers being large-scale slaveholders who later became loyal to the [Confederate States of America](/wiki/Confederate%5FStates%5Fof%5FAmerica "Confederate States of America").[\[61\]](#cite%5Fnote-61) Secretary of the Treasury [Howell Cobb](/wiki/Howell%5FCobb "Howell Cobb") was considered the greatest political talent in the Cabinet,[\[62\]](#cite%5Fnote-62) while the three department heads from the northern states were all considered to be [doughfaces](/wiki/Doughface "Doughface").[\[63\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200478-63) His objective was to dominate the cabinet, and he chose men who would agree with his views.[\[64\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200479-64) Buchanan had a troubled relationship with his vice president from the beginning, when he did not receive him during his inaugural visit but referred him to his niece and First Lady, which Breckinridge never forgave him for and saw as disrespectful.[\[65\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200486–88-65) He left out the influential [Stephen A. Douglas](/wiki/Stephen%5FA.%5FDouglas "Stephen A. Douglas"), who had made Buchanan's nomination possible by resigning at the National Convention the previous year, when filling the post.[\[66\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200480-66) Concentrating on foreign policy, he appointed the aging [Lewis Cass](/wiki/Lewis%5FCass "Lewis Cass") as Secretary of State. Buchanan's appointment of Southerners and their allies alienated many in the North, and his failure to appoint any followers of Douglas divided the party.[\[52\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200477–80-52) Outside of the cabinet, he left in place many of Pierce's appointments but removed a disproportionate number of Northerners who had ties to Democratic opponents Pierce or Douglas.[\[65\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200486–88-65) 

#### Judicial appointments

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Judicial appointments")\]

Main article: [List of federal judges appointed by James Buchanan](/wiki/List%5Fof%5Ffederal%5Fjudges%5Fappointed%5Fby%5FJames%5FBuchanan "List of federal judges appointed by James Buchanan")

Buchanan appointed one Justice, [Nathan Clifford](/wiki/Nathan%5FClifford "Nathan Clifford"), to the [Supreme Court of the United States](/wiki/Supreme%5FCourt%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Supreme Court of the United States").[\[67\]](#cite%5Fnote-67) He appointed seven other federal judges to [United States district courts](/wiki/United%5FStates%5Fdistrict%5Fcourt "United States district court"). He also appointed two judges to the [United States Court of Claims](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FCourt%5Fof%5FClaims "United States Court of Claims").[\[68\]](#cite%5Fnote-68) 

### Intervention in the _Dred Scott_ case

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Intervention in the Dred Scott case")\]

The case of _[Dred Scott v. Sandford](/wiki/Dred%5FScott%5Fv.%5FSandford "Dred Scott v. Sandford")_, to which Buchanan referred to in his inaugural address, dated back to 1846\. Scott sued for his release in [Missouri](/wiki/Missouri "Missouri"), claiming he lived in service to the proprietor in [Illinois](/wiki/Illinois "Illinois") and [Wisconsin Territory](/wiki/Wisconsin%5FTerritory "Wisconsin Territory"). The case reached the Supreme Court and gained national attention by 1856\. Buchanan consulted with Judge [John Catron](/wiki/John%5FCatron "John Catron") in January 1857, inquiring about the outcome of the case and suggesting that a broader decision, beyond the specifics of the case, would be more prudent.[\[69\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962271–272-69) Buchanan hoped that a broad decision protecting slavery in the territories could lay the issue to rest, allowing him to focus on other issues.[\[70\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200483–84-70) 

Catron replied on February 10, saying that the Supreme Court's Southern majority would decide against Scott, but would likely have to publish the decision on narrow grounds unless Buchanan could convince his fellow Pennsylvanian, Justice [Robert Cooper Grier](/wiki/Robert%5FCooper%5FGrier "Robert Cooper Grier"), to join the majority of the court.[\[71\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEHall2001566-71) Buchanan then wrote to Grier and prevailed upon him, providing the majority leverage to issue a broad-ranging decision sufficient to render the [Missouri Compromise of 1820](/wiki/Missouri%5FCompromise%5Fof%5F1820 "Missouri Compromise of 1820") unconstitutional.[\[72\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPotter1976287-72)[\[73\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200485-73) 

Two days after Buchanan was sworn in as president, Chief Justice Taney delivered the Dred Scott decision, which denied the petitioner's request to be set free from slavery. The ruling broadly asserted that Congress had no [constitutional](/wiki/U.S.%5FConstitution "U.S. Constitution") power to exclude slavery in the territories.[\[74\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962316-74) According to this decision, slaves were forever the property of their owners without rights and no African American could ever be a full citizen of the United States, even if they had full civil rights in a state.[\[75\]](#cite%5Fnote-75) Buchanan's letters were not made public at the time, but he was seen conversing quietly with the Chief Justice during his inauguration. When the decision was issued, Republicans began spreading the word that Taney had informed Buchanan of the impending outcome. Rather than destroying the Republican platform as Buchanan had hoped, the decision infuriated Northerners, who condemned it.[\[76\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200485–86-76) 

### Panic of 1857

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Panic of 1857")\]

The [Panic of 1857](/wiki/Panic%5Fof%5F1857 "Panic of 1857") began in the summer of that year, when the New York branch of [Ohio Life Insurance and Trust Company](/wiki/Ohio%5FLife%5FInsurance%5Fand%5FTrust%5FCompany "Ohio Life Insurance and Trust Company") announced its insolvency.[\[77\]](#cite%5Fnote-77) The crisis spread rapidly, and by the fall, 1,400 state banks and 5,000 businesses had gone bankrupt. Unemployment and hunger became common in northern cities, but the agricultural south was more resilient. Buchanan agreed with the southerners who attributed the economic collapse to over-speculation.[\[78\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200490-78) 

Buchanan acted in accordance with [Jacksonian Democracy](/wiki/Jacksonian%5Fdemocracy "Jacksonian democracy") principles, which restricted paper money issuance, and froze federal funds for public works projects, causing resentment among some of the population due to his refusal to implement an [economic stimulus program](/wiki/Stimulus%5F%28economics%29 "Stimulus (economics)").[\[79\]](#cite%5Fnote-79) While the government was "without the power to extend relief",[\[78\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200490-78) it would continue to pay its debts in specie, and while it would not curtail public works, none would be added. In hopes of reducing paper money supplies and inflation, he urged the states to restrict the banks to a credit level of $3 to $1 of specie and discouraged the use of federal or state bonds as security for bank note issues. The economy recovered in several years, though many Americans suffered as a result of the panic.[\[80\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962314–315-80) Buchanan had hoped to reduce the deficit, but by the time he left office the federal budget grew by 15%.[\[78\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200490-78) 

### Utah War

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Utah War")\]

Main articles: [Utah War](/wiki/Utah%5FWar "Utah War") and [Runaway Officials of 1851](/wiki/Runaway%5FOfficials%5Fof%5F1851 "Runaway Officials of 1851")

In the spring of 1857, the [Latter-day Saints](/wiki/Church%5Fof%5FJesus%5FChrist%5Fof%5FLatter-day%5FSaints "Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints") and their leader [Brigham Young](/wiki/Brigham%5FYoung "Brigham Young") had been challenging federal representatives in [Utah Territory](/wiki/Utah%5FTerritory "Utah Territory"), causing harassment and violence against non-Mormons. Young harassed federal officers and discouraged outsiders from settling in the [Salt Lake City](/wiki/Salt%5FLake%5FCity "Salt Lake City") area. In September 1857, the [Utah Territorial Militia](/wiki/Nauvoo%5FLegion#In%5FUtah "Nauvoo Legion"), associated with the Latter-day Saints, perpetrated the [Mountain Meadows massacre](/wiki/Mountain%5FMeadows%5Fmassacre "Mountain Meadows massacre"), in which Young's militia attacked a wagon train and killed 125 settlers. Buchanan was offended by the militarism and [polygamous](/wiki/Mormonism%5Fand%5Fpolygamy "Mormonism and polygamy") behavior of Young.[\[81\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200490–92-81) With reports of violence against non-Mormons, Buchanan authorized a military expedition into Utah Territory in late March 1857 to replace Young as governor. The force consisted of 2,500 men, including [Alfred Cumming](/wiki/Alfred%5FCumming%5F%28governor%29 "Alfred Cumming (governor)") and his staff, and was commanded by General [William S. Harney](/wiki/William%5FS.%5FHarney "William S. Harney"). Complicating matters, Young's notice of his replacement was not delivered because the Pierce administration had annulled the Utah mail contract, and Young portrayed the approaching forces as an unauthorized overthrow.[\[82\]](#cite%5Fnote-82)[\[74\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962316-74) 

Buchanan's personnel decision incited resistance from the Mormons around Young, as Harney was known for his volatility and brutality. In August 1857, [Albert S. Johnston](/wiki/Albert%5FSidney%5FJohnston "Albert Sidney Johnston") replaced him for organizational reasons.[\[83\]](#cite%5Fnote-83) Young reacted to the military action by mustering a two-week expedition, destroying wagon trains, oxen, and other Army property. Buchanan then dispatched [Thomas L. Kane](/wiki/Thomas%5FL.%5FKane "Thomas L. Kane") as a private agent to negotiate peace. The mission was successful, a peaceful agreement to replace Governor Young with Cumming was reached, and the [Utah War](/wiki/Utah%5FWar "Utah War") ended. The President granted amnesty to inhabitants affirming loyalty to the government, and placed the federal troops at a peaceable distance for the balance of his administration.[\[84\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962317-84) 

Buchanan did not comment on the conflict again until his State of the Union Address in December 1857, leaving open the question of whether it was a rebellion in Utah. One of Buchanan's last official acts in March 1861 was to reduce the size of Utah Territory in favor of [Nevada](/wiki/Nevada "Nevada"), [Colorado](/wiki/Colorado "Colorado"), and [Nebraska](/wiki/Nebraska "Nebraska").[\[85\]](#cite%5Fnote-85) While the Latter-day Saints had frequently defied federal authority, some historians consider Buchanan's action was an inappropriate response to uncorroborated reports.[\[74\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962316-74) 

### Transatlantic telegraph cable

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Transatlantic telegraph cable")\]

Buchanan was the first recipient of an official [telegram](/wiki/Telegram "Telegram") transmitted across the Atlantic. Following the dispatch of test and configuration telegrams, on August 16, 1858 [Queen Victoria](/wiki/Queen%5FVictoria "Queen Victoria") sent a 98-word message to Buchanan at his summer residence in the [Bedford Springs Hotel](/wiki/Bedford%5FSprings%5FHotel "Bedford Springs Hotel") in [Pennsylvania](/wiki/Pennsylvania "Pennsylvania"), expressing hope that the newly laid cable would prove "an additional link between the nations whose friendship is founded on their common interest and reciprocal esteem". Queen Victoria's message took 16 hours to send.[\[86\]](#cite%5Fnote-Trade1861-86)[\[87\]](#cite%5Fnote-bbc-saw-2-87) 

Buchanan responded: "It is a triumph more glorious, because far more useful to mankind, than was ever won by conqueror on the field of battle. May the Atlantic telegraph, under the blessing of Heaven, prove to be a bond of perpetual peace and friendship between the kindred nations, and an instrument destined by Divine Providence to diffuse religion, civilization, liberty, and law throughout the world."[\[88\]](#cite%5Fnote-spencer-vol3-88) 

### Bleeding Kansas and constitutional dispute

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Bleeding Kansas and constitutional dispute")\]

Main article: [Bleeding Kansas](/wiki/Bleeding%5FKansas "Bleeding Kansas")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/US_SlaveFree1858.gif/350px-US_SlaveFree1858.gif)](/wiki/File:US%5FSlaveFree1858.gif)

The balance of free and slave states and territories in 1858, after the admission of [Minnesota](/wiki/Minnesota "Minnesota")

The [Kansas–Nebraska Act](/wiki/Kansas%E2%80%93Nebraska%5FAct "Kansas–Nebraska Act") of 1854 created the [Kansas Territory](/wiki/Kansas%5FTerritory "Kansas Territory") and allowed the settlers there to decide whether to allow slavery. This resulted in violence between "[Free-Soil](/wiki/Free-Soil "Free-Soil")" (antislavery) and pro-slavery settlers, which developed into the "[Bleeding Kansas](/wiki/Bleeding%5FKansas "Bleeding Kansas")" period. The antislavery settlers, with the help of Northern abolitionists, organized their own territorial government in [Topeka](/wiki/Topeka "Topeka"). The more numerous proslavery settlers, many from the neighboring slave state Missouri, established a government in [Lecompton](/wiki/Lecompton,%5FKansas "Lecompton, Kansas"), giving the Territory two different governments for a time, with two distinct constitutions, each claiming legitimacy. The admission of Kansas as a state required a constitution be submitted to Congress with the approval of a majority of its residents. Under President Pierce, a series of violent confrontations escalated over who had the right to vote in Kansas. The situation drew national attention, and some in Georgia and Mississippi advocated [secession](/wiki/Secession%5Fin%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Secession in the United States") should Kansas be admitted as a free state. Buchanan chose to endorse the pro-slavery Lecompton government.[\[89\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200493–98-89) 

Buchanan appointed [Robert J. Walker](/wiki/Robert%5FJ.%5FWalker "Robert J. Walker") to replace [John W. Geary](/wiki/John%5FW.%5FGeary "John W. Geary") as Territorial Governor, and there ensued conflicting referendums from Topeka and Lecompton, where election fraud occurred. In October 1857, the Lecompton government framed the pro-slavery [Lecompton Constitution](/wiki/Lecompton%5FConstitution "Lecompton Constitution") that agreed to a referendum limited solely to the slavery question. However, the vote against slavery, as provided by the Lecompton Convention, would still permit existing slaves, and all their issue, to be enslaved, so there was no referendum that permitted the majority anti-slavery residents to prohibit slavery in Kansas. As a result, anti-slavery residents boycotted the referendum since it did not provide a meaningful choice.[\[90\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200497–100-90) 

Despite the protests of Walker and two former Kansas governors, Buchanan decided to accept the Lecompton Constitution. In a December 1857 meeting with [Stephen A. Douglas](/wiki/Stephen%5FA.%5FDouglas "Stephen A. Douglas"), the chairman of the Senate Committee on Territories, Buchanan demanded that all Democrats support the administration's position of admitting Kansas under the Lecompton Constitution. On February 2, he transmitted the Lecompton Constitution to Congress. He also transmitted a message that attacked the "revolutionary government" in Topeka, conflating them with the Mormons in Utah. Buchanan made every effort to secure congressional approval, offering favors, patronage appointments, and even cash for votes. The Lecompton Constitution won the approval of the Senate in March, but a combination of Know-Nothings, Republicans, and Northern Democrats defeated the bill in the House.[\[91\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004100–105-91) 

Buchanan never forgave Douglas, as the Northern Democrats' rejection was the deciding factor in the House's decision, and he removed all Douglas supporters from his patronage in Illinois and Washington, D.C., installing pro-administration Democrats, including postmasters.[\[92\]](#cite%5Fnote-:1-92)[\[93\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEChadwick200891-93) Rather than accepting defeat, Buchanan backed the 1858 [English Bill](/wiki/English%5FBill%5F%281858%29 "English Bill (1858)"), which offered Kansas immediate statehood and vast public lands in exchange for accepting the Lecompton Constitution. In August 1858, Kansans by referendum strongly rejected the Lecompton Constitution.[\[91\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004100–105-91) The territory received an abolitionist constitution, which was bitterly opposed in Congress by representatives and senators from the southern states until Kansas was admitted to the Union in January 1861.[\[92\]](#cite%5Fnote-:1-92) 

The dispute over Kansas became the battlefront for control of the Democratic Party. On one side were Buchanan, the majority of Southern Democrats, and the "doughfaces". On the other side were Douglas and the majority of northern Democrats, as well as a few Southerners. Douglas's faction continued to support the doctrine of popular sovereignty, while Buchanan insisted that Democrats respect the _Dred Scott_ decision and its repudiation of federal interference with slavery in the territories.[\[94\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004120–121-94) 

### 1858 mid-term elections

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: 1858 mid-term elections")\]

Douglas's Senate term was coming to an end in 1859, with the Illinois legislature, elected in 1858, determining whether Douglas would win re-election. The Senate seat was the primary issue of the legislative election, marked by the famous [debates](/wiki/Lincoln-Douglas%5Fdebates "Lincoln-Douglas debates") between Douglas and his Republican opponent for the seat, [Abraham Lincoln](/wiki/Abraham%5FLincoln "Abraham Lincoln"). Buchanan, working through federal patronage appointees in Illinois, ran candidates for the legislature in competition with both the Republicans and the Douglas Democrats. This could easily have thrown the election to the Republicans, and showed the depth of Buchanan's animosity toward Douglas.[\[95\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEChadwick2008117-95) In the end, Douglas Democrats won the legislative election and Douglas was re-elected to the Senate. In that year's elections, Douglas forces took control throughout the North, except in Buchanan's home state of Pennsylvania. Buchanan's support was otherwise reduced to a narrow base of southerners.[\[96\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEPotter1976297–327-96)[\[97\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962286–299-97) 

The division between northern and southern Democrats allowed the Republicans to win a plurality of the House in the [1858 elections](/wiki/1858%5FUnited%5FStates%5Felections "1858 United States elections"), and allowed them to block most of Buchanan's agenda. Buchanan, in turn, added to the hostility with his veto of six substantial pieces of Republican legislation.[\[98\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962312-98) Among these measures were the [Homestead Act](/wiki/Homestead%5FActs "Homestead Acts"), which would have given 160 acres of public land to settlers who remained on the land for five years, and the [Morrill Act](/wiki/Morrill%5FLand-Grant%5FActs "Morrill Land-Grant Acts"), which would have granted public lands to establish [land-grant colleges](/wiki/Land-grant%5Funiversity "Land-grant university"). Buchanan argued that these acts were unconstitutional. In the western and northwestern United States, where the Homestead Act was very popular, even many Democrats condemned the president's policies, while many Americans who considered education an important asset resented Buchanan's veto of agricultural colleges.[\[99\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004117–118-99) 

### Foreign policy

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Foreign policy")\]

Buchanan took office with an ambitious foreign policy, designed to establish U.S. [hegemony](/wiki/Hegemony "Hegemony") over Central America at the expense of Great Britain.[\[100\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTESmith197569–70-100) Buchanan sought to revitalize [Manifest Destiny](/wiki/Manifest%5Fdestiny "Manifest destiny") and to enforce the [Monroe Doctrine](/wiki/Monroe%5FDoctrine "Monroe Doctrine"), which had been under attack from the Spanish, French, and especially the British in the 1850s.[\[101\]](#cite%5Fnote-101) He hoped to re-negotiate the [Clayton–Bulwer Treaty](/wiki/Clayton%E2%80%93Bulwer%5FTreaty "Clayton–Bulwer Treaty") to counter [European imperialism](/wiki/European%5Fimperialism "European imperialism") in the Western Hemisphere, which he thought limited U.S. influence in the region. He also sought to establish American protectorates over the Mexican states of [Chihuahua](/wiki/Chihuahua%5F%28state%29 "Chihuahua (state)") and [Sonora](/wiki/Sonora "Sonora") to secure American citizens and investments, and most importantly, he hoped to achieve his long-term goal of acquiring Cuba. However, Buchanan's ambitions in Cuba and Mexico were largely blocked by the House of Representatives. After long negotiations with the British, he convinced them to cede the [Bay Islands](/wiki/Bay%5FIslands%5FDepartment "Bay Islands Department") to [Honduras](/wiki/Honduras "Honduras") and the [Mosquito Coast](/wiki/Mosquito%5FCoast "Mosquito Coast") to [Nicaragua](/wiki/Nicaragua "Nicaragua").[\[102\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004107–112-102) 

In 1858, Buchanan ordered the [Paraguay expedition](/wiki/Paraguay%5Fexpedition "Paraguay expedition") to punish [Paraguay](/wiki/Paraguay "Paraguay") for firing on the [USS _Water Witch_](/wiki/USS%5FWater%5FWitch%5F%281851%29 "USS Water Witch (1851)"), ordering 2,500 marines and 19 warships there. This costly expedition took months to reach [Asunción](/wiki/Asunci%C3%B3n "Asunción"), which successfully resulted in a Paraguayan apology and payment of an indemnity.[\[102\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004107–112-102) The chiefs of [Raiatea](/wiki/Raiatea "Raiatea") and [Tahaa](/wiki/Tahaa "Tahaa") in the South Pacific, refusing to accept the rule of King [Tamatoa V](/wiki/Tamatoa%5FV "Tamatoa V"), unsuccessfully petitioned the United States to accept the islands under a [protectorate](/wiki/Protectorate "Protectorate") in June 1858.[\[103\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEFlude2012393–413-103) Buchanan also considered buying Alaska from the [Russian Empire](/wiki/Russian%5FEmpire "Russian Empire"), as whaling in the waters there had become of great economic importance to the United States. Buchanan fueled this by spreading the rumor to the Russian ambassador [Eduard de Stoeckl](/wiki/Eduard%5Fde%5FStoeckl "Eduard de Stoeckl") in December 1857 that a large amount of Mormons intended to emigrate to Russian Alaska. In the winter of 1859, an initial purchase offer of $5,000,000 (equivalent to $169,560,000 in 2023) was made. Although the project ultimately failed due to the reservations of Foreign Minister [Alexander Gorchakov](/wiki/Alexander%5FGorchakov "Alexander Gorchakov"), the talks formed the basis for the later negotiations to purchase Alaska.[\[104\]](#cite%5Fnote-104) 

Buchanan sought trade agreements with the [Qing Dynasty](/wiki/Qing%5Fdynasty "Qing dynasty") and Japan. In China, his envoy [William Bradford Reed](/wiki/William%5FBradford%5FReed "William Bradford Reed") succeeded in having the United States included as a party to the [Treaty of Tianjin](/wiki/Treaty%5Fof%5FTientsin "Treaty of Tientsin"). In May 1860, Buchanan received a Japanese delegation consisting of several princes who carried the Harris Treaty negotiated by Townsend Harris for mutual ratification.[\[105\]](#cite%5Fnote-105) Buchanan was offered a herd of [elephants](/wiki/Elephants "Elephants") by King [Rama IV](/wiki/Rama%5FIV "Rama IV") of [Siam](/wiki/Thailand "Thailand"), though the letter arrived after Buchanan's departure from office and Buchanan's successor Abraham Lincoln declined the offer stating that the U.S. had an unsuitable climate.[\[106\]](#cite%5Fnote-106) Other presidential pets included a pair of bald eagles and a Newfoundland dog.[\[107\]](#cite%5Fnote-107) 

### Covode Committee

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Covode Committee")\]

In March 1860, the House impaneled the [Covode Committee](/wiki/United%5FStates%5FHouse%5FSelect%5FCommittee%5Fto%5FInvestigate%5FAlleged%5FCorruptions%5Fin%5FGovernment "United States House Select Committee to Investigate Alleged Corruptions in Government") to investigate the Buchanan administration's patronage system for alleged impeachable offenses, such as bribery and extortion of representatives. Buchanan supporters accused the committee, consisting of three Republicans and two Democrats, of being blatantly partisan, and claimed its chairman, Republican Rep. [John Covode](/wiki/John%5FCovode "John Covode"), was acting on a personal grudge stemming from a disputed land grant designed to benefit Covode's railroad company.[\[108\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962338-108) The Democratic committee members, as well as Democratic witnesses, were enthusiastic in their condemnation of Buchanan.[\[109\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962338–339-109)[\[110\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEGrossman200378-110) 

The committee was unable to establish grounds for impeaching Buchanan; however, the majority report issued on June 17 alleged corruption and abuse of power among members of his cabinet. The committee gathered evidence that Buchanan had tried to bribe members of Congress in his favor through intermediaries in the spring of 1858 in connection with the pro-slavery [Lecompton Constitution](/wiki/Lecompton%5FConstitution "Lecompton Constitution") of Kansas, and threatened their relatives with losing their posts if they did not vote in favor of the Lecompton Constitution.[\[110\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEGrossman200378-110) Witnesses also testified that the federal government used public funds to strengthen the intra-party faction of Douglas's opponents in Illinois.[\[111\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004114–117-111) The Democrats pointed out that evidence was scarce, but did not refute the allegations; one of the Democratic members, Rep. [James Robinson](/wiki/James%5FCarroll%5FRobinson "James Carroll Robinson"), stated that he agreed with the Republicans, though he did not sign it.[\[110\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEGrossman200378-110) 

The public was shocked by the extent of the bribery, which affected all levels and agencies of government.[\[112\]](#cite%5Fnote-112) Buchanan claimed to have "passed triumphantly through this ordeal" with complete vindication. Republican operatives distributed thousands of copies of the Covode Committee report throughout the nation as campaign material in that year's presidential election.[\[113\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004114–118-113)[\[114\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962339-114) 

### Election of 1860

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Election of 1860")\]

Main article: [1860 United States presidential election](/wiki/1860%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fpresidential%5Felection "1860 United States presidential election")

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/John_C_Breckinridge-04775-restored.jpg/170px-John_C_Breckinridge-04775-restored.jpg)](/wiki/File:John%5FC%5FBreckinridge-04775-restored.jpg)

[John C. Breckinridge](/wiki/John%5FC.%5FBreckinridge "John C. Breckinridge"), [Vice President of the United States](/wiki/Vice%5FPresident%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Vice President of the United States") under Buchanan

As he had promised in his inaugural address, Buchanan did not seek re-election. He went so far as to tell his ultimate successor, "If you are as happy in entering the White House as I shall feel on returning to Wheatland, you are a happy man."[\[115\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962-115) 

At the [1860 Democratic National Convention](/wiki/1860%5FDemocratic%5FNational%5FConventions "1860 Democratic National Conventions") in [Charleston](/wiki/Charleston,%5FSouth%5FCarolina "Charleston, South Carolina"), the party split over the issue of slavery in the territories, damaging Buchanan's reputation as the main person responsible for this issue. Though Douglas led after every ballot, he was unable to win the two-thirds majority required. The convention adjourned after 53 ballots, and re-convened in Baltimore in June. After Douglas finally won the nomination, several Southerners refused to accept the outcome, and nominated Vice President Breckinridge as their own candidate. Douglas and Breckinridge agreed on most issues except the protection of slavery. Buchanan, nursing a grudge against Douglas, failed to reconcile the party, and tepidly supported Breckinridge. With the splintering of the Democratic Party, Republican nominee [Abraham Lincoln](/wiki/Abraham%5FLincoln "Abraham Lincoln") won a four-way election that also included [John Bell](/wiki/John%5FBell%5F%28Tennessee%5Fpolitician%29 "John Bell (Tennessee politician)") of the [Constitutional Union Party](/wiki/Constitutional%5FUnion%5FParty%5F%28United%5FStates%29 "Constitutional Union Party (United States)"). Lincoln's support in the North was enough to give him an Electoral College majority. Buchanan became the last Democrat to win a presidential election until [Grover Cleveland](/wiki/Grover%5FCleveland "Grover Cleveland") in 1884.[\[116\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004118–120-116) 

As early as October, the army's [Commanding General](/wiki/Commanding%5FGeneral%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates%5FArmy "Commanding General of the United States Army"), [Winfield Scott](/wiki/Winfield%5FScott "Winfield Scott"), an opponent of Buchanan, warned him that Lincoln's election would likely cause at least seven states to secede from the union. He recommended that massive amounts of federal troops and artillery be deployed to those states to protect federal property, although he also warned that few reinforcements were available. Since 1857, Congress had failed to heed calls for a stronger militia and allowed the army to fall into deplorable condition.[\[117\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962356–358-117) Buchanan distrusted Scott and ignored his recommendations.[\[118\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200476,%5F133-118) After Lincoln's election, Buchanan directed Secretary of War [John B. Floyd](/wiki/John%5FB.%5FFloyd "John B. Floyd") to reinforce southern forts with such provisions, arms, and men as were available; however, Floyd persuaded him to revoke the order.[\[117\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962356–358-117) 

### Secession

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Secession")\]

With Lincoln's victory, talk of secession and disunion reached a boiling point, putting the burden on Buchanan to address it in his final speech to Congress on December 10\. In his message, which was anticipated by both factions, Buchanan denied the right of states to secede but maintained the federal government was without power to prevent them. He placed the blame for the crisis solely on "intemperate interference of the Northern people with the question of slavery in the Southern States," and suggested that if they did not "repeal their unconstitutional and obnoxious enactments ... the injured States, after having first used all peaceful and constitutional means to obtain redress, would be justified in revolutionary resistance to the Government of the Union."[\[119\]](#cite%5Fnote-1860message-119)[\[120\]](#cite%5Fnote-120) Buchanan's only suggestion to solve the crisis was "an explanatory amendment" affirming the constitutionality of slavery in the states, the fugitive slave laws, and [popular sovereignty](/wiki/Popular%5Fsovereignty "Popular sovereignty") in the territories.[\[119\]](#cite%5Fnote-1860message-119) His address was sharply criticized both by the North, for its refusal to stop secession, and the South, for denying its right to secede.[\[121\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962363-121) Five days after the address was delivered, Treasury Secretary [Howell Cobb](/wiki/Howell%5FCobb "Howell Cobb") resigned, as his views had become irreconcilable with the President's.[\[122\]](#cite%5Fnote-122) Even as the formation of the Confederacy by the secessionist states became increasingly apparent in the winter of 1860, the president continued to surround himself with Southerners and ignore the Republicans.[\[123\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004132–134-123) 

[![Map of U.S. showing two kinds of Union states, two phases of secession and territories](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/US_Secession_map_1861.svg/250px-US_Secession_map_1861.svg.png)](/wiki/File:US%5FSecession%5Fmap%5F1861.svg)

**Status of the states, 1861**

  States that seceded before April 15, 1861

  States that seceded after April 15, 1861

  Union states that permitted slavery

  Union states that banned slavery

  Territories

South Carolina, long the most radical Southern state, [seceded from the Union](/wiki/South%5FCarolina%5FDeclaration%5Fof%5FSecession "South Carolina Declaration of Secession") on December 20, 1860\. However, Unionist sentiment remained strong among many in the South, and Buchanan sought to appeal to the Southern moderates who might prevent secession in other states. He met with South Carolinian commissioners in an attempt to resolve the situation at [Fort Sumter](/wiki/Fort%5FSumter "Fort Sumter"), which federal forces remained in control of despite its location in Charleston, South Carolina.[\[124\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004123–134-124) Buchanan saw Congress, not himself, as responsible for finding a solution to the secession crisis. As a compromise for the southern states, Buchanan envisioned the adoption of amendments to the United States Constitution that would guarantee the right to slavery in the southern states and territories and strengthen the right of slave owners to reclaim escaped slaves as property in the northern states.[\[123\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004132–134-123) 

He refused to dismiss Interior Secretary [Jacob Thompson](/wiki/Jacob%5FThompson "Jacob Thompson") after the latter was chosen as Mississippi's agent to discuss secession, and he refused to fire Secretary of War John B. Floyd despite an embezzlement scandal. Floyd ended up resigning, but not before sending numerous firearms to Southern states, where they eventually fell into the hands of the Confederacy. Despite Floyd's resignation, Buchanan continued to seek the advice of counselors from the Deep South, including [Jefferson Davis](/wiki/Jefferson%5FDavis "Jefferson Davis") and [William Henry Trescot](/wiki/William%5FHenry%5FTrescot "William Henry Trescot").[\[124\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004123–134-124) Buchanan's friend [Rose O'Neal Greenhow](/wiki/Rose%5FO%27Neal%5FGreenhow "Rose O'Neal Greenhow") took advantage of the proximity to the president and spied for the Confederacy, which had already established a sophisticated network for gathering information from its eventual opponent before its formation.[\[123\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004132–134-123) 

Efforts were made in vain by Sen. [John J. Crittenden](/wiki/John%5FJ.%5FCrittenden "John J. Crittenden"), Rep. [Thomas Corwin](/wiki/Thomas%5FCorwin "Thomas Corwin"), and former president [John Tyler](/wiki/John%5FTyler "John Tyler") to negotiate a compromise to stop secession, with Buchanan's support. Failed attempts were also made by a group of governors meeting in New York. Buchanan secretly asked President-elect Lincoln to call for a national referendum on the issue of slavery, but Lincoln declined.[\[125\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962381–387-125) In December 1860, when the second session of the [36th Congress](/wiki/36th%5FUnited%5FStates%5FCongress "36th United States Congress") was convened, The Committee of Thirty-Three was established by the House of Representatives to prevent further states from seceding. They proposed the [Corwin Amendment](/wiki/Corwin%5FAmendment "Corwin Amendment"), which would bar Congress from interfering with slavery in states. Despite opposition from Republicans, it passed both houses of Congress and was proposed to states for ratification, but it was never ratified by the requisite number of states.[\[126\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTESmith1975152–160-126) 

Despite the efforts of Buchanan and others, six more [slave states](/wiki/Slave%5Fstates "Slave states") seceded by the end of January 1861\. Buchanan replaced the departed Southern cabinet members with [John Adams Dix](/wiki/John%5FAdams%5FDix "John Adams Dix"), [Edwin M. Stanton](/wiki/Edwin%5FM.%5FStanton "Edwin M. Stanton"), and [Joseph Holt](/wiki/Joseph%5FHolt "Joseph Holt"), all of whom were committed to preserving the Union. When Buchanan considered surrendering [Fort Sumter](/wiki/Fort%5FSumter "Fort Sumter"), the new cabinet members threatened to resign, and Buchanan relented. On January 5, Buchanan decided to reinforce Fort Sumter, sending the _[Star of the West](/wiki/Star%5Fof%5Fthe%5FWest "Star of the West")_ with 250 men and supplies. However, he failed to ask Major [Robert Anderson](/wiki/Robert%5FAnderson%5F%28Civil%5FWar%29 "Robert Anderson (Civil War)") to provide covering fire for the ship, and it was forced to return North without delivering troops or supplies. Buchanan chose not to respond to this act of war, and instead sought to find a compromise to avoid secession. He received a March 3 message from Anderson, that supplies were running low, but the response became Lincoln's to make, as the latter succeeded to the presidency the next day.[\[127\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004135–140-127) 

### States admitted to the Union

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: States admitted to the Union")\]

Three new states were [admitted to the Union](/wiki/Admission%5Fto%5Fthe%5FUnion "Admission to the Union") while Buchanan was in office:

* [Minnesota](/wiki/Minnesota "Minnesota") – May 11, 1858[\[128\]](#cite%5Fnote-128)
* [Oregon](/wiki/Oregon "Oregon") – February 14, 1859[\[129\]](#cite%5Fnote-129)
* [Kansas](/wiki/Kansas "Kansas") – January 29, 1861

## Final years and death (1861–1868)

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: Final years and death (1861–1868)")\]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/James_Buchanan_-_post_presidency.jpg/200px-James_Buchanan_-_post_presidency.jpg)](/wiki/File:James%5FBuchanan%5F-%5Fpost%5Fpresidency.jpg)

Buchanan in his later years. c. mid-1860s

After leaving office, Buchanan retired to private life in Wheatland, where he spent most of his time in his study, reading books and writing letters. The Civil War erupted within two months of Buchanan's retirement. He supported the Union and the war effort, writing to former colleagues that, "the assault upon Sumter was the commencement of war by the Confederate states, and no alternative was left but to prosecute it with vigor on our part."[\[130\]](#cite%5Fnote-Birkner-130) Buchanan supported Lincoln's introduction of universal conscription in the northern states, but was an opponent of his [Emancipation Proclamation](/wiki/Emancipation%5FProclamation "Emancipation Proclamation"). Although he recognized constitutional violations in some of the president's executive orders, he never criticized them in public.[\[131\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004142–143-131) He also wrote a letter to his fellow Pennsylvania Democrats in Harrisburg, urging them and all young men to enlist in the Union army and "join the many thousands of brave & patriotic volunteers who are already in the field."[\[130\]](#cite%5Fnote-Birkner-130) 

Buchanan was dedicated to defending his actions prior to the Civil War, which was referred to by some as "Buchanan's War".[\[130\]](#cite%5Fnote-Birkner-130) He received hate mail and threatening letters daily, and stores in Lancaster displayed Buchanan's likeness with the eyes inked red, a noose drawn around his neck and the word "TRAITOR" written across his forehead. The Senate proposed a resolution of condemnation which ultimately failed, and newspapers accused him of colluding with the Confederacy. His former cabinet members, five of whom had been given jobs in the Lincoln administration, refused to defend Buchanan publicly.[\[132\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962408–413-132) 

Buchanan became distraught by the vitriolic attacks levied against him, and fell sick and depressed. In October 1862, he defended himself in an exchange of letters with [Winfield Scott](/wiki/Winfield%5FScott "Winfield Scott"), published in the _[National Intelligencer](/wiki/National%5FIntelligencer "National Intelligencer")_.[\[133\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962417–418-133) He soon began writing his fullest public defense, in the form of his memoir _Mr. Buchanan's Administration on the Eve of Rebellion_, which was published in 1866, one year after the Civil War ended. Buchanan attributed secession to the "malign influence" of Republicans and the [abolitionist movement](/wiki/Abolitionist%5Fmovement "Abolitionist movement"). He discussed his foreign policy successes and expressed satisfaction with his decisions, even during the secession crisis. He blamed Robert Anderson, Winfield Scott, and Congress for the unresolved issue.[\[131\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004142–143-131) Two years after the publication of the memoir, Buchanan caught a [cold](/wiki/Common%5Fcold "Common cold") in May 1868, which quickly worsened due to his advanced age. He died on June 1, 1868, of [respiratory failure](/wiki/Respiratory%5Ffailure "Respiratory failure") at the age of 77 at his home at [Wheatland](/wiki/Wheatland%5F%28Lancaster%29 "Wheatland (Lancaster)"). He was interred in [Woodward Hill Cemetery](/wiki/Woodward%5FHill%5FCemetery "Woodward Hill Cemetery") in Lancaster.[\[131\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004142–143-131) 

## Political views

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Political views")\]

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_James_Buchanan_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/220px-George_Peter_Alexander_Healy_-_James_Buchanan_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg)](/wiki/File:George%5FPeter%5FAlexander%5FHealy%5F-%5FJames%5FBuchanan%5F-%5FGoogle%5FArt%5FProject.jpg)

James Buchanan (1859) by [George Healy](/wiki/George%5FHealy "George Healy") as seen in the [National Portrait Gallery](/wiki/National%5FPortrait%5FGallery%5F%28United%5FStates%29 "National Portrait Gallery (United States)") in Washington, D.C.

Buchanan was often considered by anti-slavery northerners a "[doughface](/wiki/Doughface "Doughface")", a northerner with pro-southern principles.[\[134\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEStampp199048-134) Buchanan's sympathies for the Southern states went beyond political expediency for his path to the White House. He identified with cultural and social values that he found reflected in the honor code and lifestyle of the planter class and with which he increasingly came into contact in his retirement community beginning in 1834.[\[135\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004137–138-135) Shortly after his election, he said that the "great object" of his administration was "to arrest, if possible, the agitation of the Slavery question in the North and to destroy sectional parties".[\[134\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEStampp199048-134) Although Buchanan was personally opposed to slavery,[\[24\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200430-24) he believed that the abolitionists were preventing the solution to the slavery problem. He stated, "Before \[the abolitionists\] commenced this agitation, a very large and growing party existed in several of the slave states in favor of the gradual abolition of slavery; and now not a voice is heard there in support of such a measure. The abolitionists have postponed the emancipation of the slaves in three or four states for at least half a century."[\[136\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962150-136) In deference to the intentions of the typical slaveholder, he was willing to provide the benefit of the doubt. In his third annual message to Congress, the president claimed that the slaves were "treated with kindness and humanity. ... Both the philanthropy and the self-interest of the master have combined to produce this humane result."[\[137\]](#cite%5Fnote-137) 

Buchanan thought restraint was the essence of good self-government. He believed the constitution comprised "... restraints, imposed not by arbitrary authority, but by the people upon themselves and their representatives. ... In an enlarged view, the people's interests may seem identical, but to the eye of local and sectional prejudice, they always appear to be conflicting ... and the jealousies that will perpetually arise can be repressed only by the mutual forbearance which pervades the constitution."[\[138\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962143-138) Regarding slavery and the Constitution, he stated: "Although in Pennsylvania we are all opposed to slavery in the abstract, we can never violate the constitutional compact we have with our sister states. Their rights will be held sacred by us. Under the constitution it is their own question; and there let it remain."[\[136\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962150-136)

[![James Buchanan House, 1120 Marietta Avenue, Lancaster Township](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Wheatland_-_IMG_7694.JPG/220px-Wheatland_-_IMG_7694.JPG)](/wiki/File:Wheatland%5F-%5FIMG%5F7694.JPG)

James Buchanan's home, Wheatland

One of the prominent issues of the day was [tariffs](/wiki/Tariffs%5Fin%5FUnited%5FStates%5Fhistory "Tariffs in United States history").[\[139\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEJurinski16–17-139) Buchanan was conflicted by [free trade](/wiki/Free%5Ftrade "Free trade") as well as [prohibitive tariffs](/wiki/Protective%5Ftariff "Protective tariff"), since either would benefit one section of the country to the detriment of the other. As a senator from Pennsylvania, he said: "I am viewed as the strongest advocate of protection in other states, whilst I am denounced as its enemy in Pennsylvania."[\[140\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962144-140) 

Buchanan was also torn between his desire to expand the country for the general welfare of the nation, and to guarantee the rights of the people settling particular areas. On territorial expansion, he said, "What, sir? Prevent the people from crossing the [Rocky Mountains](/wiki/Rocky%5FMountains "Rocky Mountains")? You might just as well command the [Niagara](/wiki/Niagara%5FFalls "Niagara Falls") not to flow. We must fulfill our destiny."[\[141\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962147-141) On the resulting spread of slavery, through unconditional expansion, he stated: "I feel a strong repugnance by any act of mine to extend the present limits of the Union over a new slave-holding territory." For instance, he hoped the acquisition of Texas would "be the means of limiting, not enlarging, the dominion of slavery."[\[141\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962147-141) 

## Personal life

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=31 "Edit section: Personal life")\]

Buchanan suffered from [esotropia](/wiki/Esotropia "Esotropia"). In addition, one eye was [short-sighted](/wiki/Myopia "Myopia") and the other [far-sighted](/wiki/Farsightedness "Farsightedness"). To cover this, he bent his head forward and leaned it to one side during social interactions.[\[142\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200419-142) This led to ridicule, which [Henry Clay](/wiki/Henry%5FClay "Henry Clay"), among others, used ruthlessly during a congressional debate.[\[143\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200428-143) 

In 1818, Buchanan met Anne Caroline Coleman at a grand ball in [Lancaster](/wiki/Lancaster,%5FPennsylvania "Lancaster, Pennsylvania"), and the two began courting. Anne was the daughter of the wealthy iron manufacturer [Robert Coleman](/wiki/Robert%5FColeman%5F%28industrialist%29 "Robert Coleman (industrialist)"); Robert, like Buchanan's father, was from [County Donegal](/wiki/County%5FDonegal "County Donegal") in [Ulster](/wiki/Ulster "Ulster"). Anne was also the sister-in-law of Philadelphia judge [Joseph Hemphill](/wiki/Joseph%5FHemphill "Joseph Hemphill"), one of Buchanan's colleagues. By 1819, the two were engaged, but spent little time together. Buchanan was busy with his law firm and political projects during the [Panic of 1819](/wiki/Panic%5Fof%5F1819 "Panic of 1819"), which took him away from Coleman for weeks at a time. Rumors abounded, as some suggested that he was involved with other (unidentified) women.[\[144\]](#cite%5Fnote-144) Letters from Coleman revealed she was aware of several rumors, and she accused him of only being interested in her money. She broke off the engagement, and soon afterward, on December 9, 1819, inexplicably died of "hysterical convulsions" resulting from an overdose of [laudanum](/wiki/Laudanum "Laudanum"), at the age of 23\. It was never established if the drug was taken by instruction, by accident, or by intent.[\[131\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004142–143-131)[\[145\]](#cite%5Fnote-145) Buchanan wrote to her father for permission to attend the funeral, which was refused.[\[146\]](#cite%5Fnote-146) At the time of her funeral, he said that, "I feel happiness has fled from me forever."[\[147\]](#cite%5Fnote-ReferenceA-147) Afterwards, Buchanan claimed that he remained unmarried out of devotion to his only love, who had died young.[\[131\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004142–143-131) 

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/William_Rufus_King_edited..jpg/150px-William_Rufus_King_edited..jpg)](/wiki/File:William%5FRufus%5FKing%5Fedited..jpg)

[William Rufus DeVane King](/wiki/William%5FR.%5FKing "William R. King"), Buchanan's roommate and speculated partner

In 1833 and the 1840s, he spoke of plans to marry, but these came to nothing and may merely have been due to his ambitions for a seat in the federal Senate or the White House. In the latter case, the aspirant was 19-year-old Anna Payne, the niece of former First Lady [Dolley Madison](/wiki/Dolley%5FMadison "Dolley Madison").[\[131\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004142–143-131) During his presidency, an orphaned niece, [Harriet Lane](/wiki/Harriet%5FLane "Harriet Lane"), whom he had adopted, served as official White House hostess.[\[148\]](#cite%5Fnote-148) There was an unfounded rumor that he had an affair with President Polk's widow, [Sarah Childress Polk](/wiki/Sarah%5FChildress%5FPolk "Sarah Childress Polk").[\[149\]](#cite%5Fnote-ReferenceB-149) 

Buchanan had a close relationship with [William Rufus King](/wiki/William%5FR.%5FKing "William R. King"), which became a popular target of gossip. King, like Buchanan, never married.[\[150\]](#cite%5Fnote-150) King was an Alabama politician who briefly served as vice president under [Franklin Pierce](/wiki/Franklin%5FPierce "Franklin Pierce"). Buchanan and King lived together in a Washington boardinghouse and attended social functions together from 1834 until 1844\. Such a living arrangement was then common, though Buchanan once referred to the relationship as a "communion".[\[149\]](#cite%5Fnote-ReferenceB-149) [Andrew Jackson](/wiki/Andrew%5FJackson "Andrew Jackson") mockingly called them "Miss Nancy" and "Aunt Fancy", the former being a 19th-century euphemism for an effeminate man.[\[151\]](#cite%5Fnote-151)[\[152\]](#cite%5Fnote-152) Buchanan's Postmaster General, [Aaron V. Brown](/wiki/Aaron%5FV.%5FBrown "Aaron V. Brown"), also referred to King as "Aunt Fancy", as well as Buchanan's "better half", and "wife".[\[153\]](#cite%5Fnote-153)[\[154\]](#cite%5Fnote-154)[\[155\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200475-155) King died of tuberculosis shortly after Pierce's inauguration, four years before Buchanan became president. Buchanan described him as "among the best, the purest and most consistent public men I have known".[\[149\]](#cite%5Fnote-ReferenceB-149) Biographer Baker opines that both men's nieces may have destroyed correspondence between the two men. However, she believes that their surviving letters illustrate only "the affection of a special friendship".[\[156\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200425–26-156) 

Buchanan's lifelong bachelorhood after Anne Coleman's death has drawn interest and speculation.[\[156\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200425–26-156) Some conjecture that Anne's death merely served to deflect questions about Buchanan's sexuality and bachelorhood.[\[147\]](#cite%5Fnote-ReferenceA-147) One of his biographers, Jean Baker, suggests that Buchanan was [celibate](/wiki/Celibate "Celibate"), if not [asexual](/wiki/Asexuality "Asexuality").[\[157\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker200426-157) Several writers have surmised that he was [homosexual](/wiki/Homosexual "Homosexual"), including [James W. Loewen](/wiki/James%5FW.%5FLoewen "James W. Loewen"),[\[158\]](#cite%5Fnote-158) [Robert P. Watson](/wiki/Robert%5FP.%5FWatson "Robert P. Watson"), and [Shelley Ross](/wiki/Shelley%5FRoss "Shelley Ross").[\[159\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTERoss198886–91-159)[\[160\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEWatson2012233-160) Loewen indicated that Buchanan, late in life, wrote a letter acknowledging that he might marry a woman who could accept his "lack of ardent or romantic affection".[\[161\]](#cite%5Fnote-161)[\[162\]](#cite%5Fnote-162) 

## Legacy

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Legacy")\]

### Historical reputation

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Historical reputation")\]

Though Buchanan predicted that "history will vindicate my memory,"[\[163\]](#cite%5Fnote-state%5Fpark-163) historians have criticized Buchanan for his unwillingness or inability to act in the face of secession. [Historical rankings of presidents of the United States](/wiki/Historical%5Frankings%5Fof%5Fpresidents%5Fof%5Fthe%5FUnited%5FStates "Historical rankings of presidents of the United States") without exception place Buchanan among the least successful presidents.[\[164\]](#cite%5Fnote-164) When scholars are surveyed, he ranks at or near the bottom in terms of vision/agenda-setting,[\[165\]](#cite%5Fnote-165) domestic leadership, foreign policy leadership,[\[166\]](#cite%5Fnote-166) moral authority,[\[167\]](#cite%5Fnote-167) and positive historical significance of their legacy.[\[168\]](#cite%5Fnote-168)\[_[better source needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS "Wikipedia:NOTRS")_\] According to surveys taken by American scholars and political scientists between 1948 and 1982, Buchanan ranks every time among the worst presidents of the United States, alongside [Harding](/wiki/Warren%5FG.%5FHarding "Warren G. Harding"), [Fillmore](/wiki/Millard%5FFillmore "Millard Fillmore") and [Nixon](/wiki/Richard%5FNixon "Richard Nixon").[\[169\]](#cite%5Fnote-169) 

Buchanan biographer Philip S. Klein focused in 1962, during the [Civil Rights movement](/wiki/Civil%5Frights%5Fmovement "Civil rights movement"), upon challenges Buchanan faced:

> Buchanan assumed leadership ... when an unprecedented wave of angry passion was sweeping over the nation. That he held the hostile sections in check during these revolutionary times was in itself a remarkable achievement. His weaknesses in the stormy years of his presidency were magnified by enraged partisans of the North and South. His many talents, which in a quieter era might have gained for him a place among the great presidents, were quickly overshadowed by the cataclysmic events of civil war and by the towering Abraham Lincoln.[\[170\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEKlein1962429-170)

Biographer Jean Baker is less charitable to Buchanan, saying in 2004:

> Americans have conveniently misled themselves about the presidency of James Buchanan, preferring to classify him as indecisive and inactive ... In fact Buchanan's failing during the crisis over the Union was not inactivity, but rather his partiality for the South, a favoritism that bordered on disloyalty in an officer pledged to defend all the United States. He was that most dangerous of chief executives, a stubborn, mistaken [ideologue](/wiki/Ideologue "Ideologue") whose principles held no room for compromise. His experience in government had only rendered him too self-confident to consider other views. In his betrayal of the national trust, Buchanan came closer to committing treason than any other president in American history.[\[171\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBaker2004141-171)

Other historians, such as Robert May, argued that his politics were "anything but pro-slavery",[\[172\]](#cite%5Fnote-172)[\[173\]](#cite%5Fnote-173)[\[174\]](#cite%5Fnote-174) nevertheless, a very negative view is to be found in [Michael Birkner](/wiki/Michael%5FBirkner "Michael Birkner")'s works about Buchanan.[\[175\]](#cite%5Fnote-175)[\[176\]](#cite%5Fnote-176) For [Lori Cox Han](/wiki/Lori%5FCox%5FHan "Lori Cox Han"), he ranks among scholars "as either the worst president in \[American\] history or as part of a lowest ranking failure category".[\[177\]](#cite%5Fnote-177) 

### Memorials

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=34 "Edit section: Memorials")\]

A bronze and granite [memorial](/wiki/James%5FBuchanan%5FMemorial "James Buchanan Memorial") near the southeast corner of Washington, D.C.'s [Meridian Hill Park](/wiki/Meridian%5FHill%5FPark "Meridian Hill Park") was designed by architect William Gorden Beecher and sculpted by Maryland artist [Hans Schuler](/wiki/Hans%5FSchuler "Hans Schuler"). It was commissioned in 1916 but not approved by the U.S. Congress until 1918, and not completed and unveiled until June 26, 1930\. The memorial features a statue of Buchanan, bookended by male and female classical figures representing law and diplomacy, with engraved text reading: "The incorruptible statesman whose walk was upon the mountain ranges of the law," a quote from a member of Buchanan's cabinet, [Jeremiah S. Black](/wiki/Jeremiah%5FS.%5FBlack "Jeremiah S. Black").[\[178\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEStrauss2016213-178) 

[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/BuchananmonmtDC.JPG/250px-BuchananmonmtDC.JPG)](/wiki/File:BuchananmonmtDC.JPG)

Buchanan memorial, Washington, D.C.

An earlier monument was constructed in 1907–1908 and dedicated in 1911, on the site of Buchanan's birthplace in [Stony Batter, Pennsylvania](/wiki/Buchanan%27s%5FBirthplace%5FState%5FPark "Buchanan's Birthplace State Park"). Part of the original 18.5-acre (75,000 m2) memorial site is a 250-ton pyramid structure that stands on the site of the original cabin where Buchanan was born. The monument was designed to show the original weathered surface of the native rubble and mortar.[\[179\]](#cite%5Fnote-179) 

Three counties are named in his honor, in [Iowa](/wiki/Buchanan%5FCounty,%5FIowa "Buchanan County, Iowa"), [Missouri](/wiki/Buchanan%5FCounty,%5FMissouri "Buchanan County, Missouri"), and [Virginia](/wiki/Buchanan%5FCounty,%5FVirginia "Buchanan County, Virginia"). Another in Texas was christened in 1858 but renamed [Stephens County](/wiki/Stephens%5FCounty,%5FTexas "Stephens County, Texas"), after the newly elected vice president of the Confederate States of America, [Alexander Stephens](/wiki/Alexander%5FStephens "Alexander Stephens"), in 1861.[\[180\]](#cite%5Fnote-FOOTNOTEBeatty2001310-180) The city of [Buchanan, Michigan](/wiki/Buchanan,%5FMichigan "Buchanan, Michigan"), was also named after him.[\[181\]](#cite%5Fnote-181) Several other communities are named after him: the unincorporated community of [Buchanan, Indiana](/wiki/Buchanan,%5FIndiana "Buchanan, Indiana"), the city of [Buchanan, Georgia](/wiki/Buchanan,%5FGeorgia "Buchanan, Georgia"), the town of [Buchanan, Wisconsin](/wiki/Buchanan,%5FWisconsin "Buchanan, Wisconsin"), and the townships of [Buchanan Township, Michigan](/wiki/Buchanan%5FTownship,%5FMichigan "Buchanan Township, Michigan"), and [Buchanan, Missouri](/wiki/Buchanan,%5FMissouri "Buchanan, Missouri").

[James Buchanan High School](/wiki/James%5FBuchanan%5FHigh%5FSchool "James Buchanan High School") is a small, rural high school located on the outskirts of his childhood hometown, [Mercersburg, Pennsylvania](/wiki/Mercersburg,%5FPennsylvania "Mercersburg, Pennsylvania").

### Popular culture depictions

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=James%5FBuchanan&action=edit&section=35 "Edit section: Popular culture depictions")\]

Buchanan and his legacy are central to the film _[Raising Buchanan](/wiki/Raising%5FBuchanan "Raising Buchanan")_ (2019). He is portrayed by [René Auberjonois](/wiki/Ren%C3%A9%5FAuberjonois "René Auberjonois").[\[182\]](#cite%5Fnote-182)
